Gift of Undivided Share (Musha') – Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on gift of undivided share

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on gifting an undivided share (musha'), distinguish between divisible and indivisible property, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the caliphal practice as evidence.

Core Idea
Gifting an undivided share is valid only if the property cannot be divided; the caliphs’ practice is the Hanafi proof.

The gift of an undivided share (musha') is a point of contention between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. According to the Hanafis, the ruling hinges on whether the property is divisible. If the property cannot be divided (e.g., a slave, a small bathhouse), the gift is valid; if it can be divided (e.g., a share in a house), the gift is invalid unless division actually takes place. The Shafi'i school, along with Ahmad and Malik in one narration, allows the gift in both cases, equating it to a sale. The Hanafi position is based on the established practice of the rightly-guided caliphs, who required division for the validity of a gift.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on musha'
Identify the two categories: divisible (qabil al-qismah) and indivisible (ghayr qabil). The gift is valid only for the indivisible type.
The text gives examples: slave, animal, small bathhouse (indivisible); share of a house (divisible).
2
Identify the opposing school and its view
Imam al-Shafi'i (and Ahmad and Malik in one narration) hold that the gift of musha' is valid in all cases, because it is a type of ownership transfer (tamlik) like sale, which permits undivided shares.
Their proof is qiyas on sale.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The opponent's evidence: 'It is a type of tamlik, so it is permitted like sale.' Understand why sale permits undivided shares (e.g., selling a share in a house).
In sale, undivided shares are valid because the buyer can demand division later.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond with the practice of the khulafa' rashidun, who stipulated division for validity. Also, they dismiss a hadith from Ali ('No gift without possession') as ghareeb (weak).
The caliphal precedent is considered stronger than qiyas in this case.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: In Hanafi law, gifting a share of a house without division is invalid; in Shafi'i law, it is valid. This affects how property transfers are structured.
The difference applies to divorce settlements, bequests, and everyday gifts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في محوز إلخ: يتعلق بقوله: "وتصح" أي وتصح الهبة في محوز أي مجموع قيد به احترازاً عما لو وهب الثمر على النخل بدونها، والزرع بدون الأرض ومجوز بوزن مقول اسم مفعول من حازه إذا جمعه. (فتح)
و مشاع لا يقسم أي وتصح الهبة في مشاع لا يقسم كالعبد والدابة والحمام الصغير، والقبض الكامل فيه لا يتصور، فاكتفى بالقبض القاصر، والصدقة كالهبة ثم الحد الفاصل بين ما يحتمل القسمة وما لا يحتملها: أن كل
ما كان مشتركاً بين اثنين، فطلب أحدهما القسمة، وأبى الآخر، فإن كان للقاضي أن يجبر الآبي على القسمة، فهو مما يحتملها كالدار والبيت الكبير وإن كان مما لا يجبره فهو مما لا يحتملها كالحمام. ويشترط لصحة هبة المشاع الذي لا يحتملها أن يكون قدرا معلوما، فلو وهبه نصيبه من عبد ولم يعلمه به لم يجز للجهالة. واعلم أن هبة المشاع فيما لا يقسم تفيد الملك للموهوب له على وجه لا يستحق المطالبة بالقسمة؛ لأنها لا تمكن، وأما المهاياة، فلا تجب في ظاهر الرواية؛ لأنها إعارة فإن كل واحد منهما يصير معيراً نصيبه من صاحبه، والجبر على الإعارة غير مشروع، وفي رواية: تجب. (فتح) لا فيما قسم: أي لا تصح الهبة في مشاع يقسم كسهم من الدار وقال الشافعي تجوز هبة المشاع فيما يقسم وفيما لا يقسم؛ لأنه نوع تمليك، فيجوز كالبيع، وبه قال أحمد ومالك في رواية ولنا: أن الخلفاء الراشدين شرطوا القسمة لصحة الهبة، وما روي من قوله علي: "لا تجوز الهبة إلا مقبوضة، فحديث غريب رمز الحقائق: ٢٦٠/٢]
'In a possessed' etc.: it is attached to his saying 'and valid', meaning the gift is valid in a possessed (mahuz) i.e. a collected thing, as a restriction to exclude if he gifted fruit on the palm tree without the tree, or crops without the land. 'Mahuz' is the passive participle from 'haza' when he collected. (Fath) 'And an undivided share that cannot be divided': meaning the gift is valid for an undivided share that cannot be divided, like a slave, an animal, a small bathhouse. Complete possession is not conceivable, so deficient possession suffices. Charity is like a gift. Then the boundary between what can be divided and what cannot: anything shared between two, if one requests division and the other refuses, if the judge can compel the refusing party to divide, then it is divisible like a house or large room; if not, it is not divisible like a bathhouse. For the validity of gifting an undivided share that cannot be divided, it is required that the share be known; if he gifts his share of a slave without informing him, it is not valid due to ignorance. Know that the gift of an undivided share in something indivisible confers ownership to the donee in a way that he cannot demand division because it is not possible. As for mutual allocation (muhaayah), it is not obligatory in the apparent narration because it is a loan (i'arah), as each becomes a lender of his share to the other, and compulsion to lend is not legislated; in one narration it is obligatory. (Fath) 'Not in what has been divided': i.e. the gift is not valid for an undivided share that can be divided, like a share of a house. Imam al-Shafi'i said that the gift of an undivided share is valid whether it can be divided or not, because it is a type of ownership transfer, so it is permitted like sale. Ahmad and Malik in one narration also said this. Our evidence: the rightly-guided caliphs stipulated division for the validity of a gift. And what is narrated from Ali: 'A gift is not valid unless it is taken possession of', that hadith is ghareeb. [Ramz al-Haqaiq 2:260]
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is an inter_madhhab_dispute regarding the gift of an undivided share (musha'). The Hanafi position distinguishes between musha' that cannot be divided (like a slave, a small bathhouse) and musha' that can be divided (like a share of a house). A gift of an indivisible musha' is valid, because complete possession is impossible, so deficient possession suffices. A gift of a divisible musha' is invalid, because complete possession is possible but not achieved. The Shafi'i school, and Ahmad and Malik in one narration, allow the gift of musha' in both cases, comparing it to sale which permits undivided shares. The Hanafi response (Lana) is that the practice of the rightly-guided caliphs (khulafa' rashidun) was to require division for the validity of a gift, implying that gifting an undivided share of a divisible property was not considered valid. They also mention a hadith from Ali that 'a gift is not valid unless it is taken possession of', but they classify it as ghareeb (weak/unfamiliar). The passage also defines the dividing line: if a judge can compel division upon request, the property is divisible; if not, it is indivisible. Additionally, for a valid gift of an indivisible musha', the share must be known (ma'lum), otherwise it is invalid due to ignorance. The ruling on muhaayah (mutual allocation of usufruct) is that it is not obligatory in the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah), because it would require lending, which cannot be compelled, though one narration says it is obligatory.

Key Terms
مشاع (musha'): An undivided share in joint property; ownership is not physically separated.
مهاياة (muhaayah): A mutual arrangement between co-owners to use the property in turns without dividing ownership.
غريب (ghareeb): A hadith that has only one narrator in a stage of its chain; often considered weak by Hanafis in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is gifting an undivided share of a house valid?
A) Yes, because it is a type of ownership transfer.
B) No, because the property is divisible and complete possession is possible.
C) Yes, if the donor informs the donee of the share.
D) No, unless the judge approves.

Key Takeaway
Gifting an undivided share (musha') is valid in Hanafi law only if the property is indivisible; for divisible property, physical division is required for the gift to be valid.

Gift of Divisible Property and Non-Existent Objects
Ruling on gift with division/delivery and gift of non-existent objects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of gifting an undivided share and gifting a non-existent object, and explain the role of possession (qabd) and the condition of existence.

Core Idea
A gift of an undivided share requires division and delivery; a gift of a non-existent object is void, even if it later comes into existence.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq presents two key conditions for the validity of a gift (hibah): the gift must be physically deliverable and the subject matter must exist at the time of contract. We will examine the legal reasoning behind these rules and their practical applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage states that a gift of an undivided share is valid only after division and delivery; a gift of a non-existent object is invalid, even if later extracted and delivered.
2
Understand the role of possession (qabd)
Possession is essential for completion of a gift. Division removes the impediment of commonness (shuyu') and enables possession.
3
Distinguish non-existent from attached items
Non-existent items (flour in wheat, oil in sesame) are invalid. Existing but attached items (milk in udder, wool on sheep) are like shared property; they become valid upon separation and delivery.
The key difference: attached items exist, non-existent do not.
4
Note the exception for bequest (wasiyyah)
Bequest of a non-existent item is valid because it takes effect after death when the item may exist. Gift is different because it requires immediate existence.
5
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
al-Tahawi and Qadi Khan held that delivery of a share without division does not transfer ownership; Isam held it does. The relied-upon view requires division.
This is covered in the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قسمه وسلمه صح، وإن وهب دقيقا في بر لا وإن طحن وسلم، وكذا الدهن لا تصح وصليا في السمسم، والسمن في اللبن فإن قسمه أي فإن قسمه بعد ما وهب مشاعا وسلمه صح؛ لأن تمام الهبة بالقبض، وعنده لا شيوع فيه، ولو سلم شائعا لا يملكه حتى لا ينفذ تصرفه فيه، ويكون مضموناً عليه، وينفذ فيه تصرف الواهب، ذكره الطحاوي وقاضي خان حملها، وذكر عصام أنها تفيد الملك، وبه أخذ بعض المشايخ رمز الحقائق: ٢٦٠/٢] وسلمه أي سلم الواهب الموهوب إلى الموهوب له. (محشي) وإن وهب إلخ: أي فإن وهب رجل لآخر دقيقا في بر لا تصح وإن طحن البر وسلم الدقيق، وكذا لا يصح لو وهب الدهن في السمسم أو وهب السمن في اللبن وإن استخرجهما وسلمهما؛ لأن الموهوب معدوم، إلا إذا تجدد العقد بعد استخراجها، وإنما جازت الوصية بهما؛ لأن الوصية بالمعدوم تجوز واللبن في الضرع، والصوف على ظهر الغنم والزرع والنخل في الأرض، والثمر في النخل بمنزلة المشاع؛ لأنها موجودة، وامتناع الجواز للاتصال، وذلك يعود إلى امتناع القبض كالشائع، فإذا فصلها وسلم جاز؛ لزوال المانع، كما في هبة الدين، بخلاف ما لو وهب الحمل وسلمه بعد الولادة حيث لا يجوز؛ لأن في وجوده احتمالاً، فصار كالمعدوم، والدار التي فيها المتاع، والجوالق الذي فيه الدقيق كالمشاع. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٦٠/٢]
If he divides it and delivers it, it is valid. If he gifts flour in wheat, it is not [valid] even if he grinds and delivers; similarly, oil in sesame, and ghee in milk. The explanation: 'If he divides it' means if after gifting a shared portion he divides and delivers, it is valid because the completion of the gift is with possession, and according to him there is no sharing in it. If he delivers it as shared, he does not own it such that his disposition in it is not executed, and it is guaranteed against him, and the donor's disposition in it is executed. Al-Tahawi and Qadi Khan mentioned it, and Isam mentioned that it confers ownership, and some mashayikh adopted it. [Reference: Ramz al-Haqaiq 2:260]. 'And delivers it' means the donor delivers the gifted item to the donee. (Hashiya) 'If he gifts etc.': If a man gifts flour in wheat to another, it is not valid even if he grinds the wheat and delivers the flour; similarly, it is not valid if he gifts oil in sesame or ghee in milk even if he extracts them and delivers them, because the gifted item is non-existent, unless the contract is renewed after extraction. The bequest of them is valid because bequest of a non-existent item is permissible. Milk in the udder, wool on the sheep's back, crops and palm trees in the ground, and fruit on the palm tree are like shared property because they exist, and the impediment to permissibility is attachment, which reverts to the impediment of possession like the shared portion. So if he separates them and delivers, it is permissible due to the removal of the impediment, like the gift of a debt. Unlike if he gifts a fetus and delivers it after birth, where it is not valid because its existence is uncertain, so it becomes like non-existent. The house containing furnishings and the sack containing flour are like shared property. [Reference: Ramz al-Haqaiq 2:260]
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq with its commentary covers two related rulings on gift (hibah). The first ruling: if a donor gifts an undivided share (shai') and then divides and delivers the specific portion, the gift is valid because possession (qabd) is achieved. The key condition is that the gift must not remain common; division removes the impediment of commonness. The commentary notes a difference of opinion: some early scholars, like al-Tahawi and Qadi Khan, held that delivery of a share without division does not transfer ownership, and the donor can still dispose of the property. However, others like Isam held that it does transfer ownership, and some mashayikh followed that. The second ruling: gifting a non-existent object (ma'dum) is invalid. Examples: gifting flour that is still part of wheat, oil in sesame, ghee in milk. Even if the donor later extracts the gift and delivers it, the original gift is invalid because the item was non-existent at the time of the contract. However, if a new contract is made after extraction, it is valid. The reason is that the subject matter of the gift must exist at the time of the contract. The commentary distinguishes between a gift of an inseparable part (like milk in the udder) and a bequest (wasiyyah). Bequest of a non-existent item is valid because bequest is effective after death when the item may come into existence. For a gift of an existing but attached item (like milk in udder, wool on sheep), it is similar to a shared portion: the impediment is the inability to possess separately. Once separated and delivered, the gift becomes valid. However, a fetus is different because its existence is uncertain, so it is treated as non-existent even after birth. This passage shows the Hanafi emphasis on actual possession and existence for the validity of a gift.

Key Terms
الهبة (al-hibah): A transfer of ownership without compensation (tamlik bi-ghayr 'iwad).
الشيوع (al-shuyu'): Commonness or undivided share; the state of ownership shared among multiple parties without physical division.
القبض (al-qabd): Physical or constructive possession taken by the donee.
المعدوم (al-ma'dum): That which is non-existent at the time of contract; cannot be the subject of a gift.
المشاع (al-musha'): An undivided share in a property; same as shuyu'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone gifts the milk in a cow's udder and then milks the cow and delivers the milk, is the gift valid?
A) Yes, because the milk existed and was delivered.
B) No, because the milk was non-existent at the time of the gift.
C) No, because the milk was attached at the time of the gift, and the gift is only valid if the contract is renewed after separation.
D) Yes, because the donor later separated and delivered it.

Key Takeaway
A valid gift requires the subject matter to exist and be deliverable at the time of the contract; an undivided share requires division and delivery, and a non-existent object cannot be gifted even if it later comes into existence.

Gift Without New Possession
Ruling on gift where donee already holds the item

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and apply the Hanafi rule that a gift is valid without new possession if the donee already possesses the item, and explain the principle that stronger possession substitutes for weaker.

Core Idea
Existing possession suffices; stronger possession replaces weaker, not vice versa.

This lesson clarifies a practical exception in gifts: when the donee already has the item in his hand, no fresh possession is needed. The Hanafis base this on a general principle about the interchangeability of different types of possession. Understanding this saves unnecessary formality and reflects the school's focus on substance over procedure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The text states that ownership is transferred without new possession if the item is already in the donee's hand. Note that this applies regardless of whether he held it as a trust, deposit, or guarantee.
2
Determine the condition for validity
The condition is that the donee already has physical or constructive possession at the time of the gift. The type of possession is relevant only for the principle of substitution.
3
Apply the principle of substitution
The text gives the rule: when two possessions are of the same type, one substitutes for the other; when they differ, the stronger (guaranteed possession) substitutes for the weaker, but not the reverse.
For example, possession of a usurped item (madhmun) suffices for a valid sale, but trust possession (amanah) does not suffice for a sale.
4
Analyze the examples from al-Kafi
Work through the scenarios: rescission of sale and repurchase, where the object is already in the buyer's hand as a guarantee. The possession carries over by the contract itself.
These examples reinforce the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وملك بلا قبض جديد، ولو في يد الموهوب له. وملك بلا قبض إلخ يعني يملك الموهوب له العين من غير اشتراط تجديد القبض إذا كانت في يده؛ لحصول الشرط، أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كانت في يده أمانة أو مضمونة ولو وديعة؛ لأنه بعد الهبة لم يكن عاملا للمالك، فاعتبرت يده الحقيقية، والأصل أنه متى تجانس القبضان ناب أحدهما عن الآخر، وإذا تغايرا ناب الأعلى عن الأدنى، لا عكسه، فناب قبض المغصوب والمبيع فاسدا عن قبض البيع الصحيح، ولا ينوب قبض الأمانة عنه، وفي "الكافي" من "باب" المتفرقات" : تقابضا فتقايلا، فاشترى أحدهما ما أقال صار قابضا بنفس العقد؛ لأن العرضين قائمان، فكان كل واحد مضمونا بقيمة نفسه كالمغصوب، ولو هلك أحدهما ، فتقايلا ثم جددا العقد في القائم، قابضا بنفس العقد؛ لأنه يصير مضمونا بقيمة العرض الآخر، فشابه المرهون إلخ، وذكر فروعا تتعلق يصير بالقبضين، فراجعها. [البحر الرائق: ٤١٧/٧] وهبة الأب إلخ أي بقوله : وهبت هذا الشيء لابني فلان، ولا فرق بين أن يكون هذا الشيء في يده، أو في يد مودعه، وإنما قيد بقوله : "لطفله"؛ لأنه لو وهب شيئا لابنه الكبير يشترط قبضه إن كان في عياله، ولا يكفي بقبض أبيه عندنا، وكذا إذا وهبت للطفل أمه شيئا وهو في عيالها وأبوه ميت ولا وصي له جازت الهبة. (مسكين) وإن وهب له إلخ : أي إن وهب للطفل أجنبي تتم الهبة بقبض وليه وهو الأب أو الجد أبو الأب عند عدم الأب أو وصيهما، وبقبض أمه وأجنبي لو كان الطفل في حجرهما، قيد به؛ لأنه لو لم يكن في حجرهما لا يتم بقبضهما، بخلاف الولي حيث لا يشترط كونه في حجره. وبقبضه إن عقل: يعني وتتم الهبة أيضا بقبض الطفل بنفسه إن عقل التحصيل، ولو مع وجود أبيه؛ لأنه في النافع المحض كالبالغ، حتى لو وهب له أعمى لا نفع له، وتلحقه مؤونته لا يصح قبوله، والمراد بالعقل هنا: أن يكون مميزا يعقل التحصيل، وصح رده لها كقبوله واختلف فيما لو قبض من يعوله، والأب حاضر، فقيل: لا يجوز، والصحيح هو الجواز على ما اعتمده المصنف في شرحه. (فتح)
And ownership without new possession, even if it is in the hand of the donee. 'Ownership without new possession etc.' means the donee owns the item without the condition of renewing possession if it is in his hand, because the condition is fulfilled. He expressed it generally, so it includes when it is in his hand as a trust (amanah) or as a guarantee (madhmun) even if as a deposit (wadi'ah), because after the gift he is not acting for the owner, so his actual hand is considered. The principle is that when two possessions are of the same type, one suffices for the other, and when they differ, the higher suffices for the lower, not vice versa. Thus possession of a usurped item or a corrupt sale suffices for possession of a valid sale, but possession as a trust does not suffice. In al-Kafi, from the section on miscellaneous matters: 'They exchanged possessions and then rescinded, then one of them bought what he had rescinded; he becomes a possessor by the contract itself because the two objects are present, so each is guaranteed with its own value like a usurped item. If one of them perished, and they rescinded and then renewed the contract on the existing one, they become possessors by the contract itself because it becomes guaranteed with the value of the other object, so it resembles a pawned item.' And he mentioned branches related to two possessions; refer to them. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 7:417] 'The gift of the father etc.' means by his saying: 'I gifted this thing to my son so-and-so.' There is no difference whether this thing is in his hand or in the hand of his depositary. It is restricted by his saying 'to his child' because if he gifts something to his adult son, possession is required if he is in his care, and possession by his father is not sufficient according to us. Similarly, if the mother gifts something to the child who is in her care, and his father is dead and there is no executor, the gift is valid. (Miskin) 'If a stranger gifts etc.' means if a stranger gifts to a child, the gift is completed by possession of his guardian, who is the father or the grandfather (father's father) in the absence of the father, or their executor; and by possession of the mother or a stranger if the child is in their care. It is restricted by 'if the child is in their care' because if he is not in their care, their possession does not complete it, unlike the guardian where it is not conditioned that the child be in his care. 'And by his possession if he understands' means the gift is also completed by the child's own possession if he understands acquisition (ya'qil al-tahsil), even if his father is present, because in purely beneficial matters he is like an adult. So if a blind person is gifted something that has no benefit and incurs expenses, his acceptance is not valid. What is meant by understanding here is that he is a discerning child who understands acquisition. His rejection of it is valid like his acceptance. There is a difference of opinion if one who supports him takes possession while the father is present; it is said it is not permissible, but the correct view is that it is permissible, as adopted by the author in his commentary. (Fath)
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage covers two distinct but related rules on possession (qabd) in gifts. The first rule: if the donee already has physical possession of the gifted item, no new possession is required for the gift to be complete. This applies regardless of whether the item is held as a trust (amanah), a deposit (wadi'ah), or even as a guarantee (like a usurped item). The commentary explains the Hanafi principle: when two types of possession are of the same kind, one suffices for the other; when they differ, the stronger one suffices for the weaker. For example, possession of a usurped item (which is guaranteed) is stronger and suffices for possession of a valid sale, but possession of a trust (which is not guaranteed) does not suffice for a sale. The commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq gives additional examples from al-Kafi about rescission of sale and possession. The second rule: a father's gift to his minor child is complete by the mere contract (offer and acceptance) without the need for possession. This is an exception to the general rule that possession is necessary. However, if the father gifts to his adult son who is in his care, possession is still required and the father's possession does not suffice. Similarly, a mother's gift to her minor child in her care is valid if the father is dead and no executor exists. For a stranger's gift to a minor, the gift is completed by possession taken by the child's guardian (father, grandfather, or executor), or by the mother or another caregiver if the child is in their care. The condition that the caregiver has the child in their care (hijr) is important; if the child is not in their care, their possession does not complete the gift. Additionally, if the minor is discerning (mumayyiz) and understands acquisition, his own possession completes the gift, even if his father is present, because the gift is purely beneficial. This aligns with the Hanafi position that a discerning minor can accept gifts independently. The passage also notes a difference of opinion on whether a custodian (person feeding or clothing the child) can take possession while the father is present; the correct view is that it is permissible.

Key Terms
القبض (al-qabd): Possession; taking physical or constructive control of the gifted item. Here, existing possession of any type suffices.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person holds a stolen item (usurped) and later the owner gifts it to him. Is the gift valid without new possession?
A) No, because the possession is unlawful.
B) Yes, because the item is already in his hand, and any possession suffices.
C) No, because usurpation prevents ownership.
D) Yes, but only if he returns the item first.

If the donee already has the gifted item in his possession, no new qabd is required; the existing hand fulfills the condition.

Gift to a Minor: Contract, Guardian, and Discerning Child
Rulings on gifts to minors by father, mother, stranger, and minor's own acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the different rules for gifts to a minor: father's gift is complete by contract alone; stranger's gift requires possession by guardian or discerning minor; and a discerning minor's own acceptance completes the gift.

Core Idea
A father's gift to his minor child is complete by contract; a stranger's gift needs possession by guardian or discerning minor.

Gifts to minors involve special considerations because the minor cannot fully manage property. The Hanafi school provides clear rules: the father (or his substitute) can complete the gift by mere utterance; a stranger must ensure possession is taken by a guardian or the child himself if discerning. This balance protects the child's interest while facilitating beneficial transfers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties involved
The text considers three donors: the father, the mother (in father's absence), and a stranger (non-relative). For each, different rules apply depending on the presence of a guardian and the child's discernment.
2
Understand the father's gift rule
A father's gift to his minor child is complete by the contract alone (offer and acceptance). No possession is required. This is an exception because the father is the natural guardian and his possession is considered the child's.
This applies whether the item is in the father's hand or in the hand of his depositary.
3
Distinguish the mother's gift
A mother's gift to her minor child who is in her care is valid only if the father is dead and no executor exists. The mother's possession completes the gift, but only if the child is in her hijr (care).
If the father is alive, his permission or possession is generally required, unless the mother is also a guardian.
4
Analyze stranger's gift
A stranger's gift to a minor is completed by possession taken by the minor's guardian (father, grandfather, or executor). Alternatively, the mother or a custodian can take possession if the child is in their care. If the minor is discerning (mumayyiz) and understands acquisition, his own possession completes the gift.
The condition for guardian: no requirement that child be in his care. For others (mother, custodian), the child must be in their care.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
There is a difference of opinion on whether a custodian (e.g., someone feeding the child) can take possession when the father is present. The correct view, adopted by the author, is that it is permissible.
This highlights that the school prioritizes the child's benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وملك بلا قبض جديد، ولو في يد الموهوب له. وملك بلا قبض إلخ يعني يملك الموهوب له العين من غير اشتراط تجديد القبض إذا كانت في يده؛ لحصول الشرط، أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كانت في يده أمانة أو مضمونة ولو وديعة؛ لأنه بعد الهبة لم يكن عاملا للمالك، فاعتبرت يده الحقيقية، والأصل أنه متى تجانس القبضان ناب أحدهما عن الآخر، وإذا تغايرا ناب الأعلى عن الأدنى، لا عكسه، فناب قبض المغصوب والمبيع فاسدا عن قبض البيع الصحيح، ولا ينوب قبض الأمانة عنه، وفي "الكافي" من "باب" المتفرقات" : تقابضا فتقايلا، فاشترى أحدهما ما أقال صار قابضا بنفس العقد؛ لأن العرضين قائمان، فكان كل واحد مضمونا بقيمة نفسه كالمغصوب، ولو هلك أحدهما ، فتقايلا ثم جددا العقد في القائم، قابضا بنفس العقد؛ لأنه يصير مضمونا بقيمة العرض الآخر، فشابه المرهون إلخ، وذكر فروعا تتعلق يصير بالقبضين، فراجعها. [البحر الرائق: ٤١٧/٧] وهبة الأب إلخ أي بقوله : وهبت هذا الشيء لابني فلان، ولا فرق بين أن يكون هذا الشيء في يده، أو في يد مودعه، وإنما قيد بقوله : "لطفله"؛ لأنه لو وهب شيئا لابنه الكبير يشترط قبضه إن كان في عياله، ولا يكفي بقبض أبيه عندنا، وكذا إذا وهبت للطفل أمه شيئا وهو في عيالها وأبوه ميت ولا وصي له جازت الهبة. (مسكين) وإن وهب له إلخ : أي إن وهب للطفل أجنبي تتم الهبة بقبض وليه وهو الأب أو الجد أبو الأب عند عدم الأب أو وصيهما، وبقبض أمه وأجنبي لو كان الطفل في حجرهما، قيد به؛ لأنه لو لم يكن في حجرهما لا يتم بقبضهما، بخلاف الولي حيث لا يشترط كونه في حجره. وبقبضه إن عقل: يعني وتتم الهبة أيضا بقبض الطفل بنفسه إن عقل التحصيل، ولو مع وجود أبيه؛ لأنه في النافع المحض كالبالغ، حتى لو وهب له أعمى لا نفع له، وتلحقه مؤونته لا يصح قبوله، والمراد بالعقل هنا: أن يكون مميزا يعقل التحصيل، وصح رده لها كقبوله واختلف فيما لو قبض من يعوله، والأب حاضر، فقيل: لا يجوز، والصحيح هو الجواز على ما اعتمده المصنف في شرحه. (فتح)
And ownership without new possession, even if it is in the hand of the donee. 'Ownership without new possession etc.' means the donee owns the item without the condition of renewing possession if it is in his hand, because the condition is fulfilled. He expressed it generally, so it includes when it is in his hand as a trust (amanah) or as a guarantee (madhmun) even if as a deposit (wadi'ah), because after the gift he is not acting for the owner, so his actual hand is considered. The principle is that when two possessions are of the same type, one suffices for the other, and when they differ, the higher suffices for the lower, not vice versa. Thus possession of a usurped item or a corrupt sale suffices for possession of a valid sale, but possession as a trust does not suffice. In al-Kafi, from the section on miscellaneous matters: 'They exchanged possessions and then rescinded, then one of them bought what he had rescinded; he becomes a possessor by the contract itself because the two objects are present, so each is guaranteed with its own value like a usurped item. If one of them perished, and they rescinded and then renewed the contract on the existing one, they become possessors by the contract itself because it becomes guaranteed with the value of the other object, so it resembles a pawned item.' And he mentioned branches related to two possessions; refer to them. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 7:417] 'The gift of the father etc.' means by his saying: 'I gifted this thing to my son so-and-so.' There is no difference whether this thing is in his hand or in the hand of his depositary. It is restricted by his saying 'to his child' because if he gifts something to his adult son, possession is required if he is in his care, and possession by his father is not sufficient according to us. Similarly, if the mother gifts something to the child who is in her care, and his father is dead and there is no executor, the gift is valid. (Miskin) 'If a stranger gifts etc.' means if a stranger gifts to a child, the gift is completed by possession of his guardian, who is the father or the grandfather (father's father) in the absence of the father, or their executor; and by possession of the mother or a stranger if the child is in their care. It is restricted by 'if the child is in their care' because if he is not in their care, their possession does not complete it, unlike the guardian where it is not conditioned that the child be in his care. 'And by his possession if he understands' means the gift is also completed by the child's own possession if he understands acquisition (ya'qil al-tahsil), even if his father is present, because in purely beneficial matters he is like an adult. So if a blind person is gifted something that has no benefit and incurs expenses, his acceptance is not valid. What is meant by understanding here is that he is a discerning child who understands acquisition. His rejection of it is valid like his acceptance. There is a difference of opinion if one who supports him takes possession while the father is present; it is said it is not permissible, but the correct view is that it is permissible, as adopted by the author in his commentary. (Fath)
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage covers two distinct but related rules on possession (qabd) in gifts. The first rule: if the donee already has physical possession of the gifted item, no new possession is required for the gift to be complete. This applies regardless of whether the item is held as a trust (amanah), a deposit (wadi'ah), or even as a guarantee (like a usurped item). The commentary explains the Hanafi principle: when two types of possession are of the same kind, one suffices for the other; when they differ, the stronger one suffices for the weaker. For example, possession of a usurped item (which is guaranteed) is stronger and suffices for possession of a valid sale, but possession of a trust (which is not guaranteed) does not suffice for a sale. The commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq gives additional examples from al-Kafi about rescission of sale and possession. The second rule: a father's gift to his minor child is complete by the mere contract (offer and acceptance) without the need for possession. This is an exception to the general rule that possession is necessary. However, if the father gifts to his adult son who is in his care, possession is still required and the father's possession does not suffice. Similarly, a mother's gift to her minor child in her care is valid if the father is dead and no executor exists. For a stranger's gift to a minor, the gift is completed by possession taken by the child's guardian (father, grandfather, or executor), or by the mother or another caregiver if the child is in their care. The condition that the caregiver has the child in their care (hijr) is important; if the child is not in their care, their possession does not complete the gift. Additionally, if the minor is discerning (mumayyiz) and understands acquisition, his own possession completes the gift, even if his father is present, because the gift is purely beneficial. This aligns with the Hanafi position that a discerning minor can accept gifts independently. The passage also notes a difference of opinion on whether a custodian (person feeding or clothing the child) can take possession while the father is present; the correct view is that it is permissible.

Key Terms
الولي (al-wali): Guardian of a minor, typically the father or grandfather, who can take possession on behalf of the child. For gifts, the wali's possession completes a stranger's gift.
الطفل (al-tifl): A minor child, specifically in the context of gifts. The ruling distinguishes between minor and adult children.
العقل (al-'aql): Understanding or discernment; specifically, the ability of a child to comprehend acquisition (tahsil). Required for a minor's own possession to be valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A stranger gifts a book to a 9-year-old child who is not discerning. The child's father is present but does nothing. Who must take possession for the gift to be valid?
A) The child himself, because the gift is small.
B) The father, as the guardian, must take possession.
C) The stranger must hand it directly to the child.
D) No possession is needed; the contract suffices.

Key Takeaway
A father's gift to his minor child is complete by contract alone; for others, possession by guardian or discerning minor is required.

Gift to Multiple Donees and Charity to Multiple Recipients
Ruling on multiple donors/donees and sadaqah to multiple recipients

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on gifting one item to two donees and on giving charity or a gift to two poor or rich persons, and explain the role of commonness (shuyu') and intention.

Core Idea
Gift to two donees without shares is invalid due to prohibited commonness; charity to two poor is valid because its direction is to Allah alone.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses two scenarios: multiple donors/donees in gifts, and charity/hibah to multiple recipients. In the first scenario, two donors gifting one house to one donee is valid, but one donor gifting one house to two donees is invalid according to Imam Abu Hanifa due to the creation of undivided common shares (shuyu'). The Sahibayn disagree. In the second scenario, giving charity or a gift to two poor persons is valid, but to two rich persons is not, because charity's intent is directed to Allah (who is One), while gift's intent is directed to the persons, and multiple rich recipients create commonness. This distinction has practical consequences for revocation of the gift.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two separate rulings: (1) multiple parties in gift contracts, and (2) charity/hibah to multiple recipients. First, understand the basic ruling: two donors to one donee is valid; one donor to two donees is invalid according to Abu Hanifa.
The second scenario involves sadaqah and hibah to two persons – poor vs. rich.
2
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on gift to two donees
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that gifting one item to two donees without specifying shares is invalid because it effectively gifts half to each, creating shuyu' (commonness). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue it is a transfer of ownership to each individually, so no commonness arises.
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is Abu Hanifa's view.
3
Understand the role of commonness (shuyu')
Shuyu' is prohibited in gifts because it prevents the donee from having full possession. The passage compares to sale: sale of one item to two buyers without shares is invalid by agreement because it is one contract, while gift involves two contracts (as shown by the case of one donee accepting in a non-divisible item). However, the presence of two contracts still creates commonness, hence invalidity.
Restrictions: donees must be adults, donor must be single; if two donors and two donees, invalid by agreement.
4
Apply the ruling to charity and gift to multiple recipients
Giving ten dirhams as charity (sadaqah) to two poor persons is valid, as is giving it as a gift (hibah) to two poor persons. But giving to two rich persons is invalid for both. The reason: charity is for Allah, who is One, so no commonness; gift is for the donee(s), and if multiple rich donees, commonness arises.
A gift to a poor person is metaphorically called sadaqah, and sadaqah to a rich person is metaphorically called gift, affecting the ruling on revocation.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its practical consequence
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view: the gift is invalid and the donees do not own the property unless it is divided and delivered separately. For charity to the poor, it is valid and cannot be revoked; for gift to the rich, it is invalid (or considered a different contract).
If the donor divides the item and delivers it to each donee separately, the gift becomes valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو وهب اثنان دارا لواحد صح، لا عكسه، وصح تصدق عشرة وهبتها لفقيرين لا الغني ان انا ولا منها صح: لأنهما سلماها له جملة، وهو قبضها منهما كذلك فلا شيوع رمز الحقائق: ٢٦٢/٢] (فتح) لا عكسه: قال في "البحر": أن وهو يهب واحد من اثنين كبيرين و لم يبين نصيب كل واحد عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأنه هبة النصف من كل واحد منهما بدليل أنه لو قبل أحدهما فيما لا يقسم صحت في حصته دون الآخر، فعلم أنهما عقدان، بخلاف البيع؛ فإنه لو قبل أحدهما لا يصح؛ لأنه عقد واحد، قيد بالهبة؛ لأن الرهن من رجلين، والإجارة من اثنين جائز ،اتفاقا وقيد بكون الواهب واحدا؛ لأنه لو كان ،اثنين والموهوب له كذلك، لا يجوز اتفاقا، كذا في "النهاية". وقيدنا بكون الموهوب لهما كبيرين؛ لأنه لو كان أحدهما كبيرا والآخر صغيرا، وهو في عياله، لم تجز الهبة اتفاقا؛ لأنه حين وهب صار قابضا حصة الصغير، فبقي النصف الآخر شائعا، كذا في "المحيط". [البحر الرائق: ٤٢٠/٧] قال العيني: لا يصح عكسه؛ لأن تمليك الكل منهما تمليك البعض الشائع من كل منهما؛ لأنه لا وجه له سوى هذا وهذا باطل، وقالا يجوز؛ لأن هذا تمليك واحد منهما، فلم يتحقق الشيوع، قالت الثلاثة رمز الحقائق: ٢٦٢/٢] وصح تصدق عشرة إلخ : أي يصح تصدق عشرة دراهم على فقيرين وكذا هبة عشرة دراهم على فقيرين، "لا لغنيين" أي لا يجوز التصدق بها على غنيين ولا هبتها لهما والفرق: أن الصدقة يراد بها الله وجه واحد فلا شيوع والهبة يراد بها وجه الغني وهما اثنان، والصدقة على الغني مجاز عن الهبة كما أن الهبة من الفقير مجاز عن الصدقة؛ لأن بينهما اتصالا معنويا وهو أن كل واحد منهما تمليك بغير عوض، فيجوز استعارة أحدهما للآخر، فالهبة للفقير لا توجب الرجوع، والصدقة على الغني تجوز الرجوع، وصحح في "الهداية" ما ذكر المصنف من الفرق، وهو رواية "الجامع الصغير". وقد علم بما قدمناه أن المراد من نفي الصحة هنا نفي الملك، فلو قسمها وسلمها لهما ،صحت وملكاها كما لا يخفى والله أعلم. [البحر الرائق: ٤٢٠/٧]
If two persons gift a house to one, it is valid; not the opposite. And it is valid to give charity of ten dirhams to two poor persons, and its gift [to them], not to two rich persons nor from them. 'Valid' because they both delivered it to him as a whole, and he took possession from them likewise, so there is no commonness. [Ramz al-Haqaiq 2:262] (Fath) 'Not the opposite': It is said in al-Bahr: 'If one person gifts to two adults and does not specify each one's share, according to Abu Hanifa it is not valid because it is a gift of half from each of them; the evidence is that if one of them accepts in a non-divisible item, it is valid in his share and not the other, so it is known that they are two contracts. Unlike sale, where if one accepts, it is not valid because it is one contract. It is restricted to gift because pawn from two persons and lease from two are valid by agreement. It is restricted to one donor because if there are two donors and the donee is likewise, it is not valid by agreement, as in al-Nihayah. We restricted it to the donees being adults because if one is adult and the other minor and in his care, the gift is not valid by agreement because when he gifted, he became possessor of the minor's share, so the other half remains common, as in al-Muhit.' [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 7:420] Al-'Ayni said: 'The opposite is not valid because transferring ownership of the whole to both of them is transferring ownership of a common part from each, as there is no other way, and this is invalid.' The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: 'It is valid because this is a transfer of ownership to each one of them, so commonness is not realized.' The three (imams) said [according to Ramz al-Haqaiq 2:262] 'And valid to give charity of ten etc.': It is valid to give charity of ten dirhams to two poor persons, and likewise a gift of ten dirhams to two poor persons. 'Not to two rich persons' i.e., it is not permissible to give charity to two rich persons nor to give it to them as a gift. The difference is that charity is intended for the sake of Allah, which is one direction, so no commonness; gift is intended for the sake of the rich person, and they are two. Charity to a rich person is metaphorical for a gift, just as a gift from a poor person is metaphorical for charity, because there is a spiritual connection: both are transfer of ownership without compensation, so one can be used metaphorically for the other. Thus, a gift to a poor person does not allow revocation, and charity to a rich person allows revocation. In al-Hidayah, the author's distinction is authenticated, and it is a narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir. It is known from what we have presented that what is meant by negation of validity here is negation of ownership. So if he divides it and delivers it to them, it is valid and they own it, as is not hidden. And Allah knows best. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 7:420]
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage deals with cases of multiple parties in gift contracts. The first ruling: if two donors jointly gift a single item (like a house) to one donee, the gift is valid because they deliver the whole and he takes possession as a whole, so there is no commonness (shuyu'). The opposite case—one donor giving one item to two donees without specifying shares—is invalid according to Imam Abu Hanifa, but valid according to the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). This is an intra-Hanafi dispute. Abu Hanifa's reasoning: gifting to two undividedly is effectively gifting half of the item to each, which creates a common share in each half, and gifting a common share is invalid. The evidence: if one of the two donees accepts his share in a non-divisible item, it is valid for him alone, showing that the gift is essentially two separate contracts. In contrast, sale of one item to two without specification is invalid by agreement because it is one contract. The commentators add restrictions: (1) the gift must be to two adults; if one is a minor in the donor's care, it is invalid even by agreement because the donor becomes possessor of the minor's share leaving the other half common. (2) If there are two donors and two donees, it is invalid by agreement due to commonness. The relied-upon position for fatwa is Abu Hanifa's view. The second ruling: charity (sadaqah) of ten dirhams to two poor persons is valid, as is a gift (hibah) to two poor persons. However, charity or gift to two rich persons is invalid. The reason: charity is directed to Allah, who is one, so there is no commonness; gift is directed to the donee(s), and if multiple rich donees, commonness arises. The passage further explains the conceptual relationship between sadaqah and hibah: both are transfers without compensation, so they can be metaphorical for each other. For example, a gift to a poor person is called sadaqah metaphorically, and sadaqah to a rich person is called gift. The practical consequence: a gift to a poor person does not allow the donor to revoke (return), while sadaqah to a rich person allows revocation. This distinction is authenticated in al-Hidayah. The final point: the negation of validity here means negation of ownership; if the donor divides the property and delivers it to each donee separately, the gift becomes valid.

Key Terms
الشيوع (al-shuyu'): Commonness or undivided share; when two donees own a single item without division.
الصدقة (al-sadaqah): Charitable giving for the sake of Allah, a type of gift without compensation but with spiritual reward.
الغني (al-ghani): A rich person who is not eligible for sadaqah (zakat).
الرجوع (al-ruju'): Revocation or taking back the gift by the donor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a man gives a single house to two adult brothers as a gift without specifying each one's share, is the gift valid?
A) Yes, because the brothers can share the house.
B) No, because it creates commonness (shuyu') which is invalid.
C) Yes, if the brothers agree.
D) No, unless the donor is their father.

Key Takeaway
Gifting an undivided item to multiple donees is invalid due to prohibited commonness (shuyu'); charity to multiple poor is valid because its intent is to Allah alone.

Conditions for Valid Possession by a Minor in Gifts
Conditions of custody and discernment for valid gift possession by a minor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a minor's possession of a gift (qabd) is valid and identify which cases require guardian involvement and which allow the minor to act independently.

Core Idea
A discerning minor can accept a purely beneficial gift on his own; otherwise, a guardian with actual custody must take possession.

The completion of a gift (hiba) in Hanafi fiqh requires possession (qabd). When the recipient is a minor, special rules apply to ensure the child is not burdened improperly. This lesson examines the conditions for valid possession by or on behalf of a minor, including the role of guardians, the requirement of custody, and the minor's own capacity to accept purely beneficial gifts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the possession (qabd) of a gift by or on behalf of a minor. The ruling is that it is valid (sahih) under certain conditions.
The passage confirms that possession by a guardian or by the minor himself can be valid.
2
Distinguish types of possessors
Two categories: (1) a guardian (wali) such as the father or grandfather, and (2) a custodian (man yu'ulu) who has actual care of the child. Also, the minor himself may take possession if discerning.
Guardians and custodians have different requirements; custody (hijr) is critical for non-parents.
3
Condition of custody for guardian/custodian
For a guardian who is not a parent (e.g., a stranger), possession is valid only if the child is in his actual custody (hijr). For parents, custody is assumed.
The passage states: 'and a stranger if the child is in their joint custody' – the possession is conditioned on actual custody.
4
Minor's own possession: discernment and pure benefit
A minor who is discerning (mumayyiz) can validly accept a gift himself if the gift is purely beneficial (nafi' mahd), i.e., no attached obligations like maintenance. If the gift carries a burden, the minor's acceptance is invalid.
'By his grasp if he understands' means the minor must understand the benefit of the gift.
5
Intra-madhhab ikhtilaf on custodian's possession
There is a dispute: if the child's custodian takes possession while the father is present, some say it is invalid. The correct (mufta bhi) view is permissibility, as affirmed by the author.
The author relies on the commentary of Kanz, stating 'al-sahih huwa al-jawaz'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجنبي لو في حجرهما، وبقبضه إن عقل، ولو وهب اثنان دارا لواحد صح، لا عكسه، وصح تصدق عشرة وهبتها لفقيرين لا الغني ان انا ولا منها دراهم لا يصح تصدقها هبتها (مسكين) أو وصيهما، وبقبض أمه وأجنبي لو كان الطفل في حجرهما، قيد به؛ لأنه لو لم يكن في حجرهما لا يتم بقبضهما، بخلاف الولي حيث لا يشترط كونه في حجره. وبقبضه إن عقل: يعني وتتم الهبة أيضا بقبض الطفل بنفسه إن عقل التحصيل، ولو مع وجود أبيه؛ لأنه في النافع المحض كالبالغ، حتى لو وهب له أعمى لا نفع له، وتلحقه مؤونته لا يصح قبوله، والمراد بالعقل هنا: أن يكون مميزا يعقل التحصيل، وصح رده لها كقبوله واختلف فيما لو قبض من يعوله، والأب حاضر، فقيل: لا يجوز، والصحيح هو الجواز على ما اعتمده المصنف في شرحه. (فتح)
And a stranger if [the child] is in their joint custody, and by his grasp if he understands, and if two give a house as a gift to one, it is valid, not the opposite, and it is valid to give charity of ten and its gift to two poor [people], not to two rich [people]... Some [text]... Not valid its charity or its gift. (Miskin) or their guardian, and by the grasp of his mother and a stranger if the child is in their joint custody, conditioned [by that]; because if he were not in their custody, their grasp would not complete, unlike the guardian where his being in his custody is not required. And by his grasp if he understands: meaning the gift is also completed by the grasp of the child himself if he understands the benefit, even in the presence of his father; because he, in purely beneficial matters, is like an adult, so if a blind person is given to him with no benefit and its maintenance follows him, his acceptance is not valid, and intended by understanding here: that he is discerning who understands benefit, and his rejection of it is valid like his acceptance, and it is disputed regarding if the one who supports him takes it while the father is present, some say: it is not permissible, and the correct is permissibility as affirmed by the author in his commentary. (Fath)
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage from Sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq discusses the conditions for valid possession (qabd) of a gift by a minor. The ruling establishes that a guardian can take possession on the child's behalf only if the guardian has actual custody (hijr) over the child. For parents, custody is assumed; for others, they must physically have the child under their care. Additionally, a discerning child (mumayyiz) who understands benefit can validly accept a gift himself, even without the guardian's consent, provided the gift is purely beneficial (nafi' mahd). If the gift carries obligations (like maintenance of a blind person), the child's acceptance is not valid. The author also clarifies that the child can reject a gift just as he can accept it. A point of ikhtilaf: if the child's custodian takes possession while the father is present, the correct view is permissibility.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Taking physical possession of the gifted property – an essential condition for completion of a gift.
عقل (aql): In this context, discernment (tamyiz) – the child's ability to understand benefit and detriment.
نافع محض (nafi' mahd): A gift that is purely beneficial with no attached obligations or drawbacks, e.g., money or property without maintenance costs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following statements is correct regarding a minor's acceptance of a gift?
A) The minor can accept any gift if he is discerning.
B) The guardian must have actual custody for his possession to be valid.
C) The minor cannot reject a gift once accepted.
D) The father's presence invalidates the custodian's possession.

Key Takeaway
A minor's possession of a gift is valid either by a guardian with actual custody or by the minor himself if he is discerning and the gift is purely beneficial; otherwise, only the guardian can act.

Gift from Two to One and Its Reverse
Ruling on two persons jointly gifting to one recipient and the intra-madhhab dispute on the reverse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a gift from two donors to one donee and explain the intra-madhhab dispute regarding the reverse scenario.

Core Idea
A gift from two to one is valid; from one to two is invalid per Abu Hanifa due to undivided share.

Gifting property jointly is common, but the Hanafi school has specific rules about undivided shares. The text clarifies that a gift from two donors to one recipient is valid because both hand over the entire property together. However, the reverse—one donor to two donees—triggers a dispute within the school, rooted in whether the transaction is viewed as one or two contracts. Understanding this distinction prevents invalid gifts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage addresses two gifts: (1) two donors to one donee, and (2) one donor to two donees (the reverse). Identify each.
The core ruling and ikhtilaf revolve around the reverse scenario.
2
State the ruling for each
For two donors to one donee, the gift is sahih (valid). For one donor to two donees, according to Abu Hanifa it is invalid; according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad it is valid.
The mufta bihi (operative position) is Abu Hanifa's view.
3
Understand the proof for the valid scenario
The passage states it is valid because both donors handed over the property as a whole, and the donee took possession from both, so no undivided share (shuyu') arises.
This reasoning contrasts with the reverse case.
4
Analyze the dispute: Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa sees the gift from one to two as two separate contracts (half from the donor to each donee). Evidence: if the property is indivisible and one donee accepts, the gift is valid only in his share, proving two contracts.
5
Compare with the Sahibayn's proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad view it as a single transfer of ownership to both donees, so no undivided share arises. The passage also notes consensus exceptions (e.g., mortgage from two is valid).
The practical difference: validity of the gift depends on which view is followed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صح: لأنهما سلماها له جملة، وهو قبضها منهما كذلك فلا شيوع رمز الحقائق: ٢٦٢/٢] (فتح) لا عكسه: قال في "البحر": أن وهو يهب واحد من اثنين كبيرين و لم يبين نصيب كل واحد عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأنه هبة النصف من كل واحد منهما بدليل أنه لو قبل أحدهما فيما لا يقسم صحت في حصته دون الآخر، فعلم أنهما عقدان، بخلاف البيع؛ فإنه لو قبل أحدهما لا يصح؛ لأنه عقد واحد، قيد بالهبة؛ لأن الرهن من رجلين، والإجارة من اثنين جائز ،اتفاقا وقيد بكون الواهب واحدا؛ لأنه لو كان ،اثنين والموهوب له كذلك، لا يجوز اتفاقا، كذا في "النهاية". وقيدنا بكون الموهوب لهما كبيرين؛ لأنه لو كان أحدهما كبيرا والآخر صغيرا، وهو في عياله، لم تجز الهبة اتفاقا؛ لأنه حين وهب صار قابضا حصة الصغير، فبقي النصف الآخر شائعا، كذا في "المحيط". [البحر الرائق: ٤٢٠/٧] قال العيني: لا يصح عكسه؛ لأن تمليك الكل منهما تمليك البعض الشائع من كل منهما؛ لأنه لا وجه له سوى هذا وهذا باطل، وقالا يجوز؛ لأن هذا تمليك واحد منهما، فلم يتحقق الشيوع، قالت الثلاثة رمز الحقائق: ٢٦٢/٢]
It is valid: because they both handed it over to him as a whole, and he took possession from both in that manner, so there is no undivided share. (Fath) Not its reverse: He said in 'al-Bahr': that one person giving from two adults and not specifying the share of each is, according to Abu Hanifah, invalid; because it is a gift of half from each, evidence being that if one accepted in that which is not divisible, it is valid in his share without the other, so it is known that they are two contracts, unlike sale; because if one accepts, it is not valid; because it is one contract. Restricted by 'gift' because mortgage from two men and lease from two is permissible by agreement. And restricted by the donor being one; because if there were two donors and the donee likewise, it is not permissible by agreement, as in 'al-Nihayah'. And we restricted by the donees being adults; because if one were adult and the other minor, and he is in his household, the gift is not permissible by agreement; because when he gifted, he became a possessor of the minor's share, so the remaining half remains undivided, as in 'al-Muheet'. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 7:420] Al-Ayni said: Its reverse is not valid; because making both the owners of the whole means making each owner of a part of the whole, which is not permissible. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said it is permissible; because this is giving ownership to one of them, so undivided share is not realized. The three said (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:262).
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage expands on the ruling that two donors can validly give one undivided house to one donee. The reason is that both donors hand over the entire property together, and the donee takes possession from both simultaneously, so no division is needed. However, the reverse—one donor giving an undivided property to two donees—is invalid according to Abu Hanifah because it would involve granting a share of one item to two people without specifying each donee's share, resulting in an undivided share that is not permissible. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two companions) allow it, considering it as one transfer of ownership to both. The passage also notes restrictions: if the two donors give a gift to two donees, all agree it is invalid. Also, if the donees are one adult and one minor under the donor's care, the gift is invalid by consensus because the donor becomes the minor's possessor, leaving the other half undivided.

Key Terms
شيوع (shuyū'): Undivided share or common ownership in a property that is indivisible; invalid in gift according to Abu Hanifa.
قسمة (qismah): Division of the property into shares; used here to determine if acceptance of one donee is possible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a gift from two donors to one donee valid according to the passage?
A) Because both donors are adults
B) Because the donee takes possession from both, avoiding undivided share
C) Because the two donors are relatives
D) Because the property is divisible

Key Takeaway
A gift from two to one is valid because joint transfer avoids shuyu'; the reverse from one to two is invalid per the relied-upon Hanafi position due to undivided share effect.

Sadaqa and Hiba to Two Poor vs Rich
Ruling on sadaqa and hiba to two persons, distinguishing poor from rich.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ruling on giving sadaqa or hiba to two poor vs two rich and apply the distinction to similar cases.

Core Idea
Charity to two rich is invalid because it becomes akin to a gift with undivided shares; division before delivery cures the defect.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a key rule: giving an undivided sum as charity or gift to multiple recipients is only valid if they are poor. For rich recipients, the transfer is invalid unless the property is physically divided and handed to each. The reasoning hinges on intention: charity is for Allah, gift is for the recipient's honor, and mixing undivided shares contradicts the intended nature of the transfer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
For each scenario (sadaqa/hiba to two poor/rich), state whether the transfer is valid or invalid according to the Hanafi school.
The text gives a binary ruling: valid for poor, invalid for rich.
2
Understand the reasoning (talil)
Analyze why charity to two poor is valid but to two rich is not, and why gift to poor is valid but to rich is not. Note the metaphorical relationship between sadaqa and hiba.
Charity is for Allah (one direction), so no undivided share; gift is for the rich person's face, and two faces create an undivided share.
3
Recognize the source authority
Identify that this ruling is from al-Jami' al-Saghir and confirmed by al-Hidayah, placing it in the zahir al-riwayah category.
This gives the ruling high authority within the Hanafi school.
4
Apply the condition of division
Understand that the negation of validity means negation of ownership. If the donor physically divides the property and hands it to each recipient, the transfer becomes valid and they own it.
Division cures the defect of undivided share.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح تصدق عشرة إلخ : أي يصح تصدق عشرة دراهم على فقيرين وكذا هبة عشرة دراهم على فقيرين، "لا لغنيين" أي لا يجوز التصدق بها على غنيين ولا هبتها لهما والفرق: أن الصدقة يراد بها الله وجه واحد فلا شيوع والهبة يراد بها وجه الغني وهما اثنان، والصدقة على الغني مجاز عن الهبة كما أن الهبة من الفقير مجاز عن الصدقة؛ لأن بينهما اتصالا معنويا وهو أن كل واحد منهما تمليك بغير عوض، فيجوز استعارة أحدهما للآخر، فالهبة للفقير لا توجب الرجوع، والصدقة على الغني تجوز الرجوع، وصحح في "الهداية" ما ذكر المصنف من الفرق، وهو رواية "الجامع الصغير". وقد علم بما قدمناه أن المراد من نفي الصحة هنا نفي الملك. وبه . فلو قسمها وسلمها لهما ،صحت وملكاها كما لا يخفى والله أعلم. [البحر الرائق: ٤٢٠/٧]
And it is valid to give charity of ten etc.: meaning it is valid to give charity of ten dirhams to two poor people, and likewise the gift of ten dirhams to two poor people, 'not to two rich' meaning it is not permissible to give charity to two rich people nor gift to them. And the difference: that charity is intended for Allah, so it is one direction so no undivided share, and gift is intended for the rich person's face, and they are two. And charity to the rich is metaphorical for gift, just as gift from the poor is metaphorical for charity; because there is a spiritual connection between them that each is a transfer of ownership without compensation. So one may be used metaphorically for the other. So a gift to the poor does not necessitate return, and charity to the rich permits return. And in 'al-Hidayah' it is validated what the author mentioned of the difference, and it is a narration of 'al-Jami' al-Saghir'. And it is known from what we presented that the intended meaning of negation of validity here is negation of ownership. And with it: if he divided it and handed it over to them, it is valid and they own it, as is not hidden, and Allah knows best. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 7:420]
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage clarifies the ruling that giving ten dirhams as charity (sadaqa) to two poor persons is valid, and similarly giving that amount as a gift (hiba) to two poor persons is valid. However, giving it to two rich persons, whether as charity or gift, is invalid due to the difference in intention: charity is solely for Allah's pleasure, so there is no undivided share involved when given to two; gift is intended to honor the recipient, and giving to two rich together creates an undivided share. The author notes the metaphorical relationship: calling a gift to a poor person 'charity' and charity to a rich person 'gift' is allowed due to their common element of gratuitous transfer. The ruling from al-Hidayah and al-Jami' al-Saghir is that the negation of validity means negation of ownership, i.e., the would-be donees do not own the property. However, if the donor physically divides the money and hands it to each, then it is valid and they own it.

Key Terms
صدقة (sadaqah): Charity given for the sake of Allah, cannot involve undivided share when given to multiple recipients.
هبة (hibah): Gift intended to honor the recipient; when given to multiple rich, invalid due to undivided share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is it invalid to give charity of ten dirhams to two rich persons?
A) Because rich persons are not deserving of charity
B) Because charity to multiple recipients always requires division
C) Because charity is for Allah's pleasure, giving to two rich creates undivided share
D) Because the amount is too small

Key Takeaway
Charity or gift to two rich persons is invalid unless the property is physically divided and individually delivered, because undivided shares contradict the intended nature of the transfer.

Revocation of Gifts and Attached Increase
Ruling with proof and impediments to revocation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on revocation of gifts and identify the first impediment (attached increase) that prevents revocation.

Core Idea
Revocation of a gift is valid but disliked, and an attached increase that raises the value blocks the right to revoke.

In Hanafi law, a gift transfers ownership to the donee but remains revocable by the donor after possession, albeit disliked. This chapter clarifies when revocation is valid and what prevents it. The first and most common impediment is an attached increase that enhances the gifted property's value, such as planting or building. Understanding this rule prevents donors from mistakenly thinking they can always reclaim a gift.

Guided Path
1
State the basic ruling
Identify that revocation of a gift after possession is valid (sahih) but disliked (makruh). Note the distinction between tanzihi and tahrimi dislike among scholars.
The text uses 'sihhah' (validity) rather than 'jawaz' to indicate dislike.
2
Examine the dalil
Consider the hadith narrated by the four Sunan and authenticated by al-Zayla'i: 'It is not lawful for a man to give a gift and then take it back except a father...' Observe how the Hanafi school reconciles this with another hadith about the donor's right until compensation, leading to tanzihi dislike.
The Shafi'i school takes the hadith as prohibition (tahrim), except for a father.
3
Identify the seven impediments
Recall the mnemonic 'dam' khazqah' (دمع خزقه) listing the seven preventers: attached increase, death of a party, exchange contract, income (kharaj), marriage, possession of charity, and transfer of liability (hawalah). Focus on the first: dal = ziyadah muttasilah.
Only the first impediment is explained in this passage.
4
Define attached increase
An attached increase is a physical addition that increases the property's monetary value, such as planting, building, or fattening. It does not include worthless additions or those that decrease value (e.g., a protruding tooth).
The increase must be physically attached, not separate (e.g., a child born to a slave-girl).
5
Apply the rule
Given a scenario with an attached increase, determine whether revocation is prevented. If the increase is valuable and attached, revocation is blocked. If the increase is separate (e.g., price rise) or does not increase value, revocation remains possible.
Teaching a slave a craft is disputed: Abu Yusuf says it prevents revocation; Zafar says it does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الرجوع في الهبة: لما كان حكم الهبة ثبوت الملك للموهوب له ملكا غير لازم، فكان الرجوع . وقد يمنع عن ذلك مانع، فاحتاج إلى ذكر ذلك، فهذا الباب لبيانه. (ملخص العناية) صح الرجوع: يعني صح الرجوع في الهبة بعد القبض إذا لم يمنع مانع من الموانع الآتية، والمراد من الهبة: الموهوب؛ لأن الرجوع إنما يكون في حق الأعيان لا في حق الأقوال، وأشار بذكر الصحة دون الجواز إلى أنه يكره الرجوع فيها. وظاهر كلام "المبسوط" وتبعه في "الهداية": أنها كراهة تنزيه، فإنه قال: إنه غير مستحب، ومقتضى دليل الشافعي يحله القائل بعدم الرجوع إلا فيما يهب الوالد لولده أنها كراهة تحريم وهو ما رواه أصحاب السنن الأربعة مرفوعا: "لا يحل لرجل أن يعطي عطية أو يهب هبة فيرجع فيها إلا الوالد فيما يعطي ولده ومثل الذي يعطي العطية ثم يرجع فيها كمثل الكلب يرجع في قيئه، فإنه يأكل حتى يشبع، فإذا شبع قاء، ثم عاد في قيئه"، ونقل تصحيحه الحافظ الزيلعي فإن هذا يحصل الجمع بين هذا وبين ما استدل به أئمتنا لصحته، وهو ما رواه الحاكم، وصححه مرفوعا: "من وهب هبة، فهو أحق بها ما لم يثب منها أي لم يعوض، ويدل على أنها كراهة تحريم قول الشارح: إن الرجوع قبيح، ولا يقال للمكروه تنزيها قبيح؛ لأنه من قبيل المباح أو قريب منه. وقد يقال: إن الحديث المفيد لعدم الحل محمول على ما إذا كان بغير قضاء ولا رضا كما أشار إليه في "المحيط"، وشمل كلامه ما إذا قال الواهب أسقطت حقي من الرجوع، فإنه لا يسقط، وله الرجوع، كذا في "فتاوى قاضي خان". وشمل ما إذا قال لآخر : هب لفلان عني ألف درهم، فوهب المأمور كما أمر كانت الهبة من الأمر، ولا يرجع المأمور على الآمر ، ولا على القابض، وللآمر أن يرجع في الهبة والدافع يكون متطوعا. [البحر الرائق: ٤٢١/٧] دمع خزقه: يعني منع الرجوع في الموهوب سبعة أشياء، جمعها المصنف بقوله: "دمع خزقه"، وهي مأخوذة مما قيل: ومانع من الرجوع في الهبة يا صاحبي حروف دمع خزقه فالدال الزيادة المتصلة: شرع في بيان ذلك بالفاء التفصيلية بقوله: "فالدال الزيادة المتصلة كالغرس" بالكسر " والبناء "والسمن وغيرها مما يوجب زيادة قيمة الموهوب، أما إذا لم تزد لمالكه مالية كما إذا بنى مكانا صغيرا أو غرس غرسا لا يعبأ به، فلا يسقط حق الرجوع، وكذا إذا زاد زيادة توجب نقصانه كالسن الشاخصة، فإنها ليست بزيادة حقيقة، بل هي نقصان معنى فلا تمنع الرجوع، ولو كانت الزيادة منفصلة بأن كانت الهبة أمة، فولدت عند الموهوب له من زوج، أو فجور الزنى، فللواهب أن يرجع فيها دون الولد وإن كانت الزيادة في السعر، فله أن يرجع، وإذا وهب عبدا، فعلمه القرآن أو الحرفة، لا يرجع عند أبي يوسف وفي قول زفر: يرجع، ولو ادعى
Chapter on Revocation of Gift: Since the ruling of gift is the establishment of ownership for the donee as non-binding ownership, so revocation is possible. And sometimes a preventer prevents that, so it was needed to mention that. So this chapter is for its explanation. (Mukhtasar al-Inayah) Revocation is valid: meaning revocation of the gift after possession is valid if no preventer from the coming preventers prevents it. And intended by 'gift' is the gifted object; because revocation is only with respect to physical objects, not utterances. And by mentioning validity (sihhah) instead of permissibility (jawaz) it indicates that revocation is disliked (makruh). The apparent statement of 'al-Mabsut' and followed by 'al-Hidayah' is that it is tanzihi dislike, for he said: it is not recommended. And the implication of al-Shafi'i's evidence that he holds the impossibility of revocation except for what the father gives to his child is that it is tahrimi dislike, and it is what the four Sunan narrators narrated as marfu': 'It is not lawful for a man to give a gift or donation and then take it back, except a father regarding what he gives his child. And the example of one who gives a gift and then takes it back is like a dog that returns to its vomit...' And al-Hafiz al-Zayla'i transmitted its authentication. For this achieves reconciliation between this and what our Imams used as evidence for its validity, which is what al-Hakim narrated and authenticated as marfu': 'Whoever gives a gift, he has more right to it as long as he has not been rewarded,' meaning not compensated. And it indicates that it is tahrimi dislike based on the commentator's statement that revocation is ugly (qabih), and it is not said for tanzihi disliked that it is ugly, because it is from the permissible category or close to it. And it may be said: the hadith indicating non-permissibility is interpreted as when it is without a decree and consent, as indicated in 'al-Muheet'. And his statement included the case where the donor says 'I have dropped my right to revocation,' then it does not drop, and he has the right to revoke, as in 'Fatawa Qadi Khan'. And it included when he says to another: 'Give a thousand dirhams as a gift from me to so-and-so,' then the agent gives as commanded, the gift is from the committer, and the agent does not have recourse against the committer nor against the recipient, and the committer has the right to revoke the gift, and the payer is a volunteer. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 7:421] 'Dam' Khazqah: meaning seven things prevent revocation of the gifted object, the author compiled them in his statement: 'Dam' Khazqah'. They are taken from what is said: And the preventer of revocation in the gift, O my friend, are the letters of 'Dam' Khazqah'. Then the Dal: the attached increase: he began its explanation with the detail letter Fa' saying: 'So the Dal is the attached increase like planting' (with kasra on the ra) 'and building and fatness and other things that cause an increase in the value of the gifted object. As for when it does not increase its monetary value, like if he built a small place or planted a worthless plant, the right of revocation does not fall. Similarly, if an increase occurs that causes a decrease, like a protruding tooth, it is not a real increase but a decrease in meaning, so it does not prevent revocation. And if the increase was separate, like the gift was a slave-girl, and she gave birth at the donee's place from a husband or from fornication, then the donor can revoke her but not the child. And if the increase was in price, he can revoke. And if he gave a slave as a gift and taught him the Qur'an or a craft, Abu Yusuf says he cannot revoke, and in Zafar's statement: he can revoke, and if he claims...
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on revocation of gifts. It establishes that revocation is valid (sahih) after possession, but is disliked (makruh). There is a difference among scholars over the degree of dislike: most Hanafis consider it tanzihi (not recommended), while al-Shafi'i considers it tahrimi (prohibited) based on a hadith comparing a revoking donor to a dog returning to its vomit. The Hanafis reconcile this with the hadith that the donor has the most right until compensation is given, indicating the dislike is not prohibition. The passage then lists the seven preventers of revocation using the mnemonic 'دمع خزقه' (dam' khazqah). Here only the first (dal) is explained: attached increase (al-ziyadah al-muttasilah) such as planting, building, fattening, etc., that increases the value. If the increase does not increase value (worthless) or reduces value (e.g., a protruding tooth), it does not prevent revocation. Separate increase (e.g., a child born to the donee) does not prevent revocation of the mother but the donor cannot revoke the child. Teaching a slave the Qur'an or a craft is disputed: Abu Yusuf says it prevents revocation; Zafar says it does not.

Key Terms
رجوع (rujū'): Revocation of a gift by the donor, taking back ownership of the gifted object.
زيادة متصلة (ziyādah muttasiLah): An increase that is physically attached to the gifted property (e.g., planting, building, fattening) that raises its value, preventing revocation.
دمع خزقه (dam' khazqah): Mnemonic for seven impediments to revocation in Hanafi law: Dal (ziyādah muttasilah), Mim (mawt), 'Ayn ( 'aqd mu'āwaḍah ), Kha (kharāj), Zay (zawaj), Qaf (qabd ṣadaqah), Hā (hawalah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an attached increase that prevents revocation of a gift according to the Hanafi school?
A) A child born to a slave-girl
B) An increase in the market price
C) Building a house on the gifted land
D) Teaching the gifted slave a craft

Key Takeaway
Revocation of a gift is valid but disliked (tanzihi), and an attached increase that enhances value permanently blocks the right to revoke.
